<div class="page-hero">
  <div class="eyebrow">COGS 160 · Track 2 of 4</div>
  <h1>Track TWO: AI Article Pipeline</h1>
  <p>
    Build automated article discovery systems using AI. Implement RAG-based retrieval, relevance scoring, and Value of Information prioritization to continuously find and filter research articles.
  </p>
</div>

<div class="outer">

  <div class="content-section">
    <h2 class="section-title">Track Overview</h2>
    <p class="section-intro">
      This track is not about manually finding more citations. It's about automating the article discovery process using artificial intelligence and information theory.
    </p>

    <div class="callout callout-info">
      <strong>The Goal:</strong> Build a system that continuously hunts for relevant papers and presents them to other students ranked by how much they'd contribute to filling knowledge gaps. You'll combine Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG), relevance filtering, and Value of Information (VOI) scoring.
    </div>

    <h3 style="font-family:Georgia,serif;font-size:1.05rem;color:var(--navy);margin-top:28px;margin-bottom:12px;">Core Skills You'll Develop</h3>
    <ul>
      <li><strong>Automated Retrieval:</strong> Query engines that connect to Semantic Scholar API and other open-access sources</li>
      <li><strong>RAG Systems:</strong> Retrieval-Augmented Generation pipelines that rank articles by relevance to existing knowledge</li>
      <li><strong>Relevance Filtering:</strong> Distinguish experimental articles from theory papers and meta-reviews</li>
      <li><strong>Value of Information:</strong> Prioritize which articles would most advance the knowledge base</li>
      <li><strong>Quality Assurance:</strong> Automated gates that verify article type, topic relevance, and data quality</li>
      <li><strong>Benchmarking:</strong> Test and measure retrieval accuracy against human-selected articles</li>
    </ul>

    <h3 style="font-family:Georgia,serif;font-size:1.05rem;color:var(--navy);margin-top:28px;margin-bottom:12px;">Weekly Syllabus</h3>

    <table class="milestone-table">
      <thead>
        <tr>
          <th>Weeks</th>
          <th>Phase</th>
          <th>Activities & Deliverables</th>
        </tr>
      </thead>
      <tbody>
        <tr>
          <td class="week-col">1–2</td>
          <td class="phase-col">Article Collection (A0)</td>
          <td class="activity-col">Upload 10 experiment articles + 10 best articles from Google Scholar (everyone does this)</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td class="week-col">3–4</td>
          <td class="phase-col">Basic Query Engine</td>
          <td class="activity-col">Connect to Semantic Scholar API. Build query builder. Retrieve 50 candidate articles for assigned topics. Write data validation spec.</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td class="week-col">5–6</td>
          <td class="phase-col">RAG & Relevance Scoring</td>
          <td class="activity-col">Implement RAG using embeddings. Build relevance classifier (experiment vs. theory). Test on 100 articles. Measure F1 score.</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td class="week-col">7–8</td>
          <td class="phase-col">VOI & Prioritization</td>
          <td class="activity-col">Integrate Value of Information weighting. Rank articles by likely contribution to gaps. Build automated quality gates. Benchmark against manual selection.</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td class="week-col">9–10</td>
          <td class="phase-col">Final Pipeline & Integration</td>
          <td class="activity-col">Polish pipeline, write operational documentation, integrate into Fall 160 site. Course-wide debugging.</td>
        </tr>
      </tbody>
    </table>

    <div class="callout callout-info" style="margin-top:28px;">
      <strong>Key Concepts to Understand:</strong>
      <ul style="margin-top:10px;margin-left:1.2rem;">
        <li><strong>RAG:</strong> Retrieval-Augmented Generation — combining vector search with LLMs to rank articles contextually</li>
        <li><strong>Relevance:</strong> Not just "mentions keywords," but "is an empirical study on topic" vs. "is a review or theory paper"</li>
        <li><strong>Value of Information:</strong> Which articles would fill the most critical gaps in the knowledge base?</li>
        <li><strong>Semantic Scholar API:</strong> Open-access, powerful tool for academic article retrieval</li>
        <li><strong>Embedding-based search:</strong> Using language models to understand semantic similarity between papers</li>
      </ul>
    </div>

  </div>

  <!-- ── Join Track ── -->
  <div id="join-track-section" style="text-align:center;margin:2rem 0;padding:1.5rem;background:#f0f7f5;border:2px solid #1C3D3A;border-radius:12px;">
    <p style="font-size:1rem;margin-bottom:1rem;color:#1C3D3A;font-weight:600;">Ready to commit to this track?</p>

    <div id="join-track-msg" style="display:none;margin-top:1rem;padding:1rem;border-radius:8px;font-weight:600;"></div>
  </div>

<script>
async function joinTrack(trackId) {
  const KA_AUTH_API = window.__KA_CONFIG__?.apiBase ?? '';
  const token = localStorage.getItem('ka_access_token');
  const btn = document.getElementById('join-track-btn');
  const msg = document.getElementById('join-track-msg');
  const trackNames = { track1: 'Track 1: Image Tagging', track2: 'Track 2: AI Article Pipeline', track3: 'Track 3: VR for Experiments', track4: 'Track 4: Interaction Design & Evaluation' };
  if (!token) { msg.style.display='block'; msg.style.background='#fff3cd'; msg.style.color='#856404'; msg.textContent='Please sign in first to join a track.'; return; }
  btn.disabled = true; btn.textContent = 'Joining…';
  try {
    const res = await fetch(KA_AUTH_API + '/auth/update-track', { method: 'POST', headers: { 'Content-Type': 'application/json', 'Authorization': 'Bearer ' + token }, body: JSON.stringify({ track: trackId }) });
    const data = await res.json();
    if (res.ok) {
      msg.style.display='block'; msg.style.background='#d4edda'; msg.style.color='#155724';
      msg.innerHTML = '&#10003; ' + data.message + '<br><small>This is now shown on your profile. You can change tracks later if needed.</small>';
      btn.textContent = '&#10003; Joined!'; btn.style.background = '#27ae60';
      try { const u = JSON.parse(localStorage.getItem('ka_current_user') || '{}'); u.track = trackId; localStorage.setItem('ka_current_user', JSON.stringify(u)); } catch(e) {}
    } else {
      msg.style.display='block'; msg.style.background='#f8d7da'; msg.style.color='#721c24'; msg.textContent = data.detail || 'Failed to join track.';
      btn.disabled = false; btn.textContent = 'Join ' + trackNames[trackId];
    }
  } catch(e) {
    msg.style.display='block'; msg.style.background='#f8d7da'; msg.style.color='#721c24'; msg.textContent = 'Could not reach the server. Is ka_auth_server.py running?';
    btn.disabled = false; btn.textContent = 'Join ' + trackNames[trackId];
  }
}
(function() {
  try {
    const u = JSON.parse(localStorage.getItem('ka_current_user') || '{}');
    if (u.track === 'track2' || u.trackChoice1 === 'track2') {
      const msg = document.getElementById('join-track-msg');
      msg.style.display='block'; msg.style.background='#d4edda'; msg.style.color='#155724';
      msg.innerHTML = '&#10003; You are currently in Track 2: AI Article Pipeline';
      document.getElementById('join-track-btn').textContent = 'Switch to Track 2 (already joined)';
      document.getElementById('join-track-btn').style.background = '#27ae60';
    }
  } catch(e) {}
})();
</script>

<div class="page-hero">
  <div class="eyebrow">COGS 160 · Track 3 of 4</div>
  <h1>Track THREE: VR for Experiments</h1>
  <p>
    Create a library of configurable VR environments for controlled experiments. Build parametric scenes where ceiling height, lighting, materials, and other variables can be adjusted to match real experimental needs.
  </p>
</div>

<div class="outer">

  <div class="content-section">
    <h2 class="section-title">Track Overview</h2>
    <p class="section-intro">
      Built environments constrain what experiments are possible. But running experiments in real buildings is costly and slow.
      Your track creates a collection of reusable VR environments that experimentalists can customize to run the studies they need.
    </p>

    <div class="callout callout-info">
      <strong>The Goal:</strong> Build a library of configurable VR environments that can be rapidly modified to match experimental protocols. Each environment should be packaged as a reusable template with clear documentation so researchers can run controlled studies in virtual scenes.
    </div>

    <h3 style="font-family:Georgia,serif;font-size:1.05rem;color:var(--navy);margin-top:28px;margin-bottom:12px;">Why VR for Built Environment Research?</h3>
    <ul>
      <li><strong>Control:</strong> Real buildings have hundreds of confounding variables. VR lets you isolate individual factors (lighting, layout, materials) that would be impossible to control in the field.</li>
      <li><strong>Replicability:</strong> Run the exact same environment across multiple subjects. Impossible in the real world.</li>
      <li><strong>Scale:</strong> Test dozens of environment variations quickly and cheaply. A real building takes months and millions of dollars.</li>
      <li><strong>Measurement:</strong> Integrate eye tracking, physiological sensors, and behavioral data capture directly into the VR environment.</li>
      <li><strong>Customization:</strong> Researchers can modify parametric values (ceiling height, window size, material colors) without rebuilding the environment.</li>
    </ul>

    <h3 style="font-family:Georgia,serif;font-size:1.05rem;color:var(--navy);margin-top:28px;margin-bottom:12px;">Core Skills You'll Develop</h3>
    <ul>
      <li><strong>VR Fundamentals:</strong> 3D scene setup, coordinate systems, scale, collision, and physics</li>
      <li><strong>Parametric Design:</strong> Creating environments where key variables can be adjusted via parameters</li>
      <li><strong>Experimental Integration:</strong> Eye tracking, physiological data capture, behavioral measurement</li>
      <li><strong>Procedural Generation:</strong> Using AI to help rapidly generate environment variants</li>
      <li><strong>Documentation & Templates:</strong> Making environments reusable by other researchers</li>
    </ul>

    <h3 style="font-family:Georgia,serif;font-size:1.05rem;color:var(--navy);margin-top:28px;margin-bottom:12px;">Weekly Syllabus</h3>

    <table class="milestone-table">
      <thead>
        <tr>
          <th>Weeks</th>
          <th>Phase</th>
          <th>Activities & Deliverables</th>
        </tr>
      </thead>
      <tbody>
        <tr>
          <td class="week-col">1–2</td>
          <td class="phase-col">VR Fundamentals</td>
          <td class="activity-col">Set up Unity or Unreal. Create basic architectural scene. Understand coordinate systems, scale for humans, collision.</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td class="week-col">3–4</td>
          <td class="phase-col">Parametric Environments</td>
          <td class="activity-col">Build configurable room where ceiling height, window size, lighting type, material colors can be adjusted via parameters. Create design specification document.</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td class="week-col">5–6</td>
          <td class="phase-col">Experimental Instrumentation</td>
          <td class="activity-col">Integrate eye tracking. Add measurement points for behavioral coding. Build data logging system. Create 3 dependent variable measurements.</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td class="week-col">7–8</td>
          <td class="phase-col">AI-Assisted Generation & Testing</td>
          <td class="activity-col">Use procedural/AI tools to generate environment variants. Run naïve-user walkthroughs. Collect usability feedback. Iterate on design.</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td class="week-col">9–10</td>
          <td class="phase-col">Template Documentation & Integration</td>
          <td class="activity-col">Package environment as reusable template. Write researcher-facing documentation. Integrate into Fall 160 site. Course-wide debugging.</td>
        </tr>
      </tbody>
    </table>

    <div class="callout callout-info" style="margin-top:28px;">
      <strong>Tools:</strong> You'll use either <strong>Unity</strong> (C#, industry standard, larger ecosystem) or <strong>Unreal Engine</strong> (C++, more powerful rendering). Both are free for educational use. Talk to the instructor about which fits your experience level.
    </div>

    <div class="callout callout-info">
      <strong>Key Concepts:</strong>
      <ul style="margin-top:10px;margin-left:1.2rem;">
        <li><strong>Parametric Design:</strong> Building scenes where key variables are exposed as adjustable parameters</li>
        <li><strong>Dependent Variables:</strong> What will you measure? Eye gaze? Head position? Dwell time? Heart rate?</li>
        <li><strong>Experimental Validity:</strong> Does the VR environment faithfully represent real architectural conditions?</li>
        <li><strong>Usability Testing:</strong> Can researchers actually use and configure the environment?</li>
        <li><strong>Replicability:</strong> Can someone else run your environment and get consistent results?</li>
      </ul>
    </div>

  </div>

    <div id="join-track-msg" style="display:none;margin-top:1rem;padding:1rem;border-radius:8px;font-weight:600;"></div>
  </div>

<script>
async function joinTrack(trackId) {
  const KA_AUTH_API = window.__KA_CONFIG__?.apiBase ?? '';
  const token = localStorage.getItem('ka_access_token');
  const btn = document.getElementById('join-track-btn');
  const msg = document.getElementById('join-track-msg');
  const trackNames = { track1: 'Track 1: Image Tagging', track2: 'Track 2: AI Article Pipeline', track3: 'Track 3: VR for Experiments', track4: 'Track 4: Interaction Design & Evaluation' };
  if (!token) { msg.style.display='block'; msg.style.background='#fff3cd'; msg.style.color='#856404'; msg.textContent='Please sign in first to join a track.'; return; }
  btn.disabled = true; btn.textContent = 'Joining…';
  try {
    const res = await fetch(KA_AUTH_API + '/auth/update-track', { method: 'POST', headers: { 'Content-Type': 'application/json', 'Authorization': 'Bearer ' + token }, body: JSON.stringify({ track: trackId }) });
    const data = await res.json();
    if (res.ok) {
      msg.style.display='block'; msg.style.background='#d4edda'; msg.style.color='#155724';
      msg.innerHTML = '&#10003; ' + data.message + '<br><small>This is now shown on your profile. You can change tracks later if needed.</small>';
      btn.textContent = '&#10003; Joined!'; btn.style.background = '#27ae60';
      try { const u = JSON.parse(localStorage.getItem('ka_current_user') || '{}'); u.track = trackId; localStorage.setItem('ka_current_user', JSON.stringify(u)); } catch(e) {}
    } else {
      msg.style.display='block'; msg.style.background='#f8d7da'; msg.style.color='#721c24'; msg.textContent = data.detail || 'Failed to join track.';
      btn.disabled = false; btn.textContent = 'Join ' + trackNames[trackId];
    }
  } catch(e) {
    msg.style.display='block'; msg.style.background='#f8d7da'; msg.style.color='#721c24'; msg.textContent = 'Could not reach the server. Is ka_auth_server.py running?';
    btn.disabled = false; btn.textContent = 'Join ' + trackNames[trackId];
  }
}
(function() {
  try {
    const u = JSON.parse(localStorage.getItem('ka_current_user') || '{}');
    if (u.track === 'track3' || u.trackChoice1 === 'track3') {
      const msg = document.getElementById('join-track-msg');
      msg.style.display='block'; msg.style.background='#d4edda'; msg.style.color='#155724';
      msg.innerHTML = '&#10003; You are currently in Track 3: VR for Experiments';
      document.getElementById('join-track-btn').textContent = 'Switch to Track 3 (already joined)';
      document.getElementById('join-track-btn').style.background = '#27ae60';
    }
  } catch(e) {}
})();
</script>

<div class="page-hero">
  <div class="eyebrow">COGS 160 · Track 4 of 4</div>
  <h1>Track FOUR: Interaction Design & Evaluation</h1>
  <p>
    Understand how different user types navigate Knowledge Atlas. Analyze their information needs, improve interaction design, and measure whether the system actually works for them.
  </p>
</div>

<div class="outer">

  <div class="content-section">
    <h2 class="section-title">Track Overview</h2>
    <p class="section-intro">
      Knowledge Atlas is complex. We need to personalize it for different user types because a student explorer's journey is very different from an instructor's, or a researcher's, or a theory explorer's.
      Your track is about understanding those differences and making the system better for each user type.
    </p>

    <div class="callout callout-info">
      <strong>The Goal:</strong> Map out how different user types navigate the K-Atlas system. Identify missing CTAs, dead ends, information overload, and misalignments between user needs and page structure. Then test improvements with real users and measure success.
    </div>

    <h3 style="font-family:Georgia,serif;font-size:1.05rem;color:var(--navy);margin-top:28px;margin-bottom:12px;">User Types We Support</h3>

    <div class="user-type-grid">
      <div class="user-type-card">
        <div class="user-type-name">Student Explorer</div>
        <div class="user-type-desc">Learning the field. Wants overview of topics, propositions, evidence. Navigates by curiosity.</div>
      </div>
      <div class="user-type-card">
        <div class="user-type-name">Researcher</div>
        <div class="user-type-desc">Testing hypotheses. Needs deep access to evidence, methodologies, caveats. Searches by topic/methodology.</div>
      </div>
      <div class="user-type-card">
        <div class="user-type-name">Contributor</div>
        <div class="user-type-desc">Adding to K-Atlas. Needs to understand schema, propose new warrants, upload evidence. Oriented toward creation.</div>
      </div>
      <div class="user-type-card">
        <div class="user-type-name">Instructor</div>
        <div class="user-type-desc">Teaching with K-Atlas. Needs to understand propositions deeply, curate content for courses, track student engagement.</div>
      </div>
      <div class="user-type-card">
        <div class="user-type-name">Practitioner</div>
        <div class="user-type-desc">Applying K-Atlas. Wants actionable guidance on built environment design decisions based on evidence.</div>
      </div>
      <div class="user-type-card">
        <div class="user-type-name">Theory Explorer</div>
        <div class="user-type-desc">Studying epistemology. Interested in warrant structures, evidence dynamics, coherence metrics.</div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <h3 style="font-family:Georgia,serif;font-size:1.05rem;color:var(--navy);margin-top:28px;margin-bottom:12px;">Core Skills You'll Develop</h3>
    <ul>
      <li><strong>User Research:</strong> Conducting interviews and creating personas for different user types</li>
      <li><strong>Journey Mapping:</strong> Visualizing the path a user takes through K-Atlas to accomplish their goal</li>
      <li><strong>Information Architecture:</strong> Understanding what content each user type needs and when</li>
      <li><strong>Cognitive Load Analysis:</strong> Identifying where pages are overwhelming or confusing</li>
      <li><strong>Usability Testing:</strong> Running tests with real users, measuring task completion rates</li>
      <li><strong>Interaction Design:</strong> Improving CTAs, navigation, search, filtering, content structure</li>
    </ul>

    <h3 style="font-family:Georgia,serif;font-size:1.05rem;color:var(--navy);margin-top:28px;margin-bottom:12px;">Weekly Syllabus</h3>

    <table class="milestone-table">
      <thead>
        <tr>
          <th>Weeks</th>
          <th>Phase</th>
          <th>Activities & Deliverables</th>
        </tr>
      </thead>
      <tbody>
        <tr>
          <td class="week-col">1–2</td>
          <td class="phase-col">User Type Analysis</td>
          <td class="activity-col">Interview or create personas for all 6 user types. Document: What questions does each user have? What's their goal? What frustrates them?</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td class="week-col">3–4</td>
          <td class="phase-col">Journey Mapping</td>
          <td class="activity-col">Map current user journeys for each type. Where do they get stuck? Where are missing CTAs? Review <code>docs/USER_JOURNEY_AUDIT_2026-04-01.md</code>.</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td class="week-col">5–6</td>
          <td class="phase-col">Information Architecture Audit</td>
          <td class="activity-col">For each user type: What content do they need at each step? Is cognitive load too high? Where are dead ends? Design improvements.</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td class="week-col">7–8</td>
          <td class="phase-col">Prototype & Implement</td>
          <td class="activity-col">Implement CTA improvements, navigation fixes, content reorganization. Create improved page layouts or features. Commit to git.</td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
          <td class="week-col">9–10</td>
          <td class="phase-col">Usability Testing & Iteration</td>
          <td class="activity-col">Test with real users (5-8 per user type). Measure task completion rates. Document findings. Iterate on design. Course-wide integration.</td>
        </tr>
      </tbody>
    </table>

    <div class="callout callout-info" style="margin-top:28px;">
      <strong>Starting Point:</strong> There's already a user journey audit at <code>docs/USER_JOURNEY_AUDIT_2026-04-01.md</code> in the repo. Start there to see what gaps have already been identified.
    </div>

    <div class="callout callout-info">
      <strong>Key Metrics:</strong>
      <ul style="margin-top:10px;margin-left:1.2rem;">
        <li><strong>Task Completion Rate:</strong> % of users who complete their goal without getting lost</li>
        <li><strong>Time to Goal:</strong> How long does it take to accomplish the core task?</li>
        <li><strong>Cognitive Load:</strong> Do users feel overwhelmed or confused?</li>
        <li><strong>Information Findability:</strong> Can users find the content they need?</li>
        <li><strong>CTA Effectiveness:</strong> Do navigation buttons and links guide users to the right place?</li>
      </ul>
    </div>

  </div>

<script>
async function joinTrack(trackId) {
  const KA_AUTH_API = window.__KA_CONFIG__?.apiBase ?? '';
  const token = localStorage.getItem('ka_access_token');
  const btn = document.getElementById('join-track-btn');
  const msg = document.getElementById('join-track-msg');
  const trackNames = { track1: 'Track 1: Image Tagging', track2: 'Track 2: AI Article Pipeline', track3: 'Track 3: VR for Experiments', track4: 'Track 4: Interaction Design & Evaluation' };
  if (!token) { msg.style.display='block'; msg.style.background='#fff3cd'; msg.style.color='#856404'; msg.textContent='Please sign in first to join a track.'; return; }
  btn.disabled = true; btn.textContent = 'Joining…';
  try {
    const res = await fetch(KA_AUTH_API + '/auth/update-track', { method: 'POST', headers: { 'Content-Type': 'application/json', 'Authorization': 'Bearer ' + token }, body: JSON.stringify({ track: trackId }) });
    const data = await res.json();
    if (res.ok) {
      msg.style.display='block'; msg.style.background='#d4edda'; msg.style.color='#155724';
      msg.innerHTML = '&#10003; ' + data.message + '<br><small>This is now shown on your profile. You can change tracks later if needed.</small>';
      btn.textContent = '&#10003; Joined!'; btn.style.background = '#27ae60';
      try { const u = JSON.parse(localStorage.getItem('ka_current_user') || '{}'); u.track = trackId; localStorage.setItem('ka_current_user', JSON.stringify(u)); } catch(e) {}
    } else {
      msg.style.display='block'; msg.style.background='#f8d7da'; msg.style.color='#721c24'; msg.textContent = data.detail || 'Failed to join track.';
      btn.disabled = false; btn.textContent = 'Join ' + trackNames[trackId];
    }
  } catch(e) {
    msg.style.display='block'; msg.style.background='#f8d7da'; msg.style.color='#721c24'; msg.textContent = 'Could not reach the server. Is ka_auth_server.py running?';
    btn.disabled = false; btn.textContent = 'Join ' + trackNames[trackId];
  }
}
(function() {
  try {
    const u = JSON.parse(localStorage.getItem('ka_current_user') || '{}');
    if (u.track === 'track4' || u.trackChoice1 === 'track4') {
      const msg = document.getElementById('join-track-msg');
      msg.style.display='block'; msg.style.background='#d4edda'; msg.style.color='#155724';
      msg.innerHTML = '&#10003; You are currently in Track 4: Interaction Design & Evaluation';
      document.getElementById('join-track-btn').textContent = 'Switch to Track 4 (already joined)';
      document.getElementById('join-track-btn').style.background = '#27ae60';
    }
  } catch(e) {}
})();
</script>

  <main class="main">
    <div class="page-eyebrow">COGS 160 · Spring Quarter</div>
    <h1 class="page-title">Article Finder Assignment</h1>
    <p class="page-subtitle">
      Find ten peer-reviewed empirical papers that address your assigned K-ATLAS search question,
      build a relevance filter that automates the screening decision, validate it against your
      own manual judgments, and contribute both the papers and the filter to the K-ATLAS corpus.
    </p>

    <!-- OVERVIEW -->
    <section id="overview">
      <h2>What Is This Assignment?</h2>
      <p>
        K-ATLAS's knowledge base grows by ingesting peer-reviewed papers about
        environment–behavior relationships. Each paper that enters the system adds evidence
        that can update the credence values the system assigns to theoretical claims —
        for example, raising the default credence (DC) of the claim that natural environments
        reduce amygdala activation, or strengthening the warrant connecting fractal complexity
        to stress recovery. But the system needs a way to decide, quickly and reliably,
        whether a given paper is <em>relevant</em> to a particular search question.
        That is where you come in.
      </p>
      <p>
        Your assignment has two interlocking parts. The first is a systematic article search:
        using Google AI Citation, Elicit, Consensus, and related tools, you will find ten
        peer-reviewed, empirical papers not currently in K-ATLAS that directly address your
        assigned search question. The second is a technical contribution: you will build a
        <em>relevance filter</em> — a Python program that takes a search question and a paper's
        abstract and outputs a relevance score and binary judgment. You will calibrate this
        filter against your own manual labels, validate it on a held-out set, and integrate
        it into K-ATLAS as a callable function.
      </p>
      <p>
        Both parts depend on each other. The filter is calibrated on papers you find
        during your search. The filter, in turn, helps you screen candidates quickly
        during extended searches. Understanding what the filter gets right and wrong
        will deepen your understanding of what relevance means in this domain.
      </p>

      <div class="callout teal">
        <div class="callout-title">Individual work</div>
        <p>The article finder assignment is completed individually — unlike the image tagger,
        which is a group project. You claim one question from the K-ATLAS question bank
        (questions marked <strong>available</strong>), and that question becomes yours
        for the duration of the assignment. No two students work on the same question.</p>
      </div>
    </section>

    <!-- INTELLECTUAL CHALLENGE -->
    <section id="intellectual-challenge">
      <h2>The Intellectual Challenge</h2>
      <p>
        Relevance is not a binary property of papers — it is a relational judgment that
        depends on the specificity of the question, the evidence type sought, and the
        theoretical frame being used. A paper that reports cortisol reduction after
        a 20-minute walk in a forest is relevant to a question about physiological
        stress recovery in natural environments, but may or may not be relevant to a
        question specifically about Stress Recovery Theory, depending on whether the
        paper engages SRT as a theoretical frame or merely reports the finding.
        A paper on aquatic soundscapes and relaxation might be relevant to a question
        about water feature design, but irrelevant to one specifically asking about
        <em>visual</em> features.
      </p>
      <p>
        This is precisely why building a relevance filter is intellectually interesting:
        you must make these distinctions explicit. The act of defining what counts as
        relevant — precisely enough that a program can reproduce your judgment — forces
        clarity about the question's scope and the theoretical claims it is trying to support.
        You will discover that some of your initial intuitions about relevance are inconsistent,
        and resolving those inconsistencies is part of the work.
      </p>
    </section>

    <!-- QUEUE PRIORITY -->
    <section id="queue-priority">
      <h2>How the Question Queue Works</h2>
      <p>
        K-ATLAS begins with 130 search questions. As students complete assignments —
        each submission represents approximately ten new papers for a given question —
        the marginal value of additional submissions for that question diminishes.
        Most of the high-profile, easily-discoverable papers for a well-trodden question
        will already be in the corpus after the first two or three submissions.
        A fourth student working on the same question faces a harder search task
        and produces less new value for the system.
      </p>
      <p>
        For this reason, the question bank is a <em>priority queue</em>: questions with
        zero completed submissions are listed first and are flagged as high priority.
        Questions with one or two completed submissions are listed next. Questions with
        three or more completed submissions appear at the end of the queue and are
        flagged as lower priority — not because the question is unimportant, but because
        the corpus coverage for that question is already substantial.
      </p>
      <p>
        When you log in to the student portal and browse available questions, you will
        see a priority badge next to each question. Claiming a high-priority question
        is better for K-ATLAS and, practically, means a more open search landscape —
        fewer papers to rule out as already-in-corpus. The queue updates in real time
        as other students submit, so check it before finalising your choice.
      </p>
      <div class="callout tip">
        <div class="callout-title">Queue priority badges</div>
        <p><strong>🟢 HIGH</strong> — 0 completed submissions. Open territory; most good papers are available.<br>
        <strong>🟡 MEDIUM</strong> — 1–2 completed submissions. Still worthwhile; expect ~20–30% corpus overlap.<br>
        <strong>🔴 LOW</strong> — 3+ completed submissions. Hard mode; most accessible papers already taken.<br>
        <strong>✅ COMPLETE</strong> — 10+ completed submissions. Only available for extra-credit bonus work.</p>
      </div>
    </section>

    <!-- KATLAS TOOLS -->
    <section id="katlas-tools">
      <h2>Two Tools K-ATLAS Provides</h2>
      <p>
        Rather than building all search and filtering infrastructure from scratch,
        K-ATLAS provides two scripts that handle the heaviest parts of the pipeline.
        You are expected to use both, understand what each does, and report on their
        output in your reflection report. The relevance filter <em>you build</em>
        sits inside this infrastructure as the question-specific scoring layer.
      </p>

      <h3>1. <code>auto_search.py</code> — Automatic paper discovery</h3>
      <p>
        Given your question ID, this script queries the Semantic Scholar and OpenAlex
        academic APIs and returns a ranked CSV of candidate papers. It performs
        lightweight TF-IDF relevance scoring, checks each DOI against the K-ATLAS corpus,
        and flags open-access papers with downloadable PDFs. Use it at the start of
        your search to get an initial candidate pool quickly.
      </p>
      <div class="code-block">
        python3 scripts/auto_search.py \\
            --question-id SQ-031 \\
            --db ./ae.db \\
            --output ./search_results/SQ-031_candidates.csv
      </div>
      <p>
        The output CSV is sorted by relevance score (new-to-corpus papers first).
        Open-access papers include a direct PDF URL in the <code>open_access_url</code>
        column — download those PDFs for processing in the next step.
        The script is a starting point, not a complete solution. It typically returns
        15–25 candidates per run; you should also search manually using Google AI
        Citation and Elicit to find papers that APIs miss.
      </p>

      <h3>2. <code>process_submitted_pdfs.py</code> — PDF drop and review table</h3>
      <p>
        This is the core workflow tool. Once you have a folder of PDF candidates —
        from auto-search downloads, from Google AI Citation, from Elicit, or from
        your library — drop them all into a folder and run this script. It reads
        each PDF, extracts the title, DOI, and abstract automatically, checks the
        DOI against the K-ATLAS corpus, runs a three-stage relevance assessment,
        and outputs a review table with a recommendation for each paper.
      </p>
      <div class="code-block">
        python3 scripts/process_submitted_pdfs.py \\
            --folder ~/Downloads/my_candidate_pdfs/ \\
            --question-id SQ-031 \\
            --db ./ae.db
      </div>
      <p>
        The output table has one row per PDF and the following key columns:
        <code>in_corpus</code> (yes/no — papers already in K-ATLAS cannot count toward your ten),
        <code>question_score</code> (cosine similarity of the PDF's abstract and title to your assigned question),
        <code>domain_score</code> (maximum cosine similarity across all 130 K-ATLAS questions — indicates whether the paper is in the right field even if not your specific question),
        <code>is_empirical</code> (heuristic detection of methods-section language),
        and <code>recommendation</code> (SUBMIT / REVIEW / SKIP).
      </p>
      <p>
        The script uses both the abstract <em>and</em> the full PDF text. The abstract
        is used for relevance scoring (it is the most concentrated statement of the paper's
        contribution). The full text is used for the empirical detector — methods-section
        language (sample sizes, statistical terms, data collection descriptions) appears
        in the body, not the abstract. This dual-source approach catches cases where a
        paper has a relevant abstract but is actually a commentary or theoretical essay
        without data.
      </p>
      <div class="callout info">
        <div class="callout-title">What you build vs. what K-ATLAS provides</div>
        <p>The provided scripts handle domain filtering (Pass 1: is this paper in our field?)
        and empirical detection (Pass 3: does this paper report data?).
        The relevance filter <strong>you build</strong> is Pass 2: the question-specific
        scoring layer calibrated to your assigned question. Your filter is what makes
        the pipeline precise for your question specifically — it is also what
        gets integrated into K-ATLAS's infrastructure permanently.</p>
      </div>
    </section>

    <!-- WEEK 1 -->
    <section id="week1">
      <div class="week-header">
        <span class="week-badge">Week 1</span>
        <span class="week-title">Claim Your Question and Begin Searching</span>
        <span class="week-dates">Days 1–7</span>
      </div>
      <div class="week-body">
        <p>
          Log in to K-ATLAS and claim a question from the available question bank,
          choosing from the high-priority end of the queue. Read your question carefully
          several times before beginning any search. Ask yourself: what exactly is being
          asked? What kind of evidence would answer it most directly — an RCT, an fMRI
          study, a post-occupancy survey, a meta-analysis? What theory does it anchor to,
          and what would a paper need to say to be genuinely relevant rather than merely
          topically adjacent?
        </p>
        <p>
          You have two search modes, and you should use both during the week.
        </p>
        <p>
          <em>Automatic mode</em>: run <code>auto_search.py</code> with your question ID.
          This queries Semantic Scholar and OpenAlex and returns a ranked CSV of
          15–25 candidates within a minute. Download the open-access PDFs listed
          in the output for processing in Week 2. Record the session in your search log
          with <code>query_variant = original</code> and <code>tool = SemanticScholar</code>
          or <code>OpenAlex</code>.
        </p>
        <p>
          <em>Manual mode</em>: read the <a href="teach-google-search.html">Google AI Citation
          teaching module</a> in full, then run your assigned query verbatim in Google
          and examine the AI Overview citations. Open each cited paper in a new tab,
          read the abstract, and make a quick relevance judgment. Download the PDFs
          of candidate papers. Run additional searches in Elicit and Consensus.
          Record every session in your search log — including rejected papers.
        </p>
        <p>
          By the end of Week 1, your PDF candidate folder should contain at least
          20 PDFs and you should have a search log covering at least three sessions
          across at least two tools. These PDFs feed directly into the Week 2 workflow.
        </p>
        <div class="callout tip">
          <div class="callout-title">Collect PDFs, not just titles</div>
          <p>The PDF drop workflow in Week 2 requires actual PDF files. During Week 1,
          whenever you find a candidate paper, download the PDF immediately — via the
          open-access URL, your library proxy, Unpaywall, or the author's page. It is
          much harder to track down PDFs later than to grab them when you first encounter
          the paper.</p>
        </div>
      </div>
    </section>

    <!-- WEEK 2 -->
    <section id="week2">
      <div class="week-header">
        <span class="week-badge">Week 2</span>
        <span class="week-title">PDF Drop, Review Table, and Build Your Filter</span>
        <span class="week-dates">Days 8–14</span>
      </div>
      <div class="week-body">
        <p>
          At the start of Week 2, run the PDF processor on your full candidate folder:
        </p>
        <div class="code-block">
          python3 scripts/process_submitted_pdfs.py \\
              --folder ~/my_candidate_pdfs/ \\
              --question-id SQ-031 \\
              --db ./ae.db
        </div>
        <p>
          The script produces a review table — a CSV with one row per PDF, showing
          whether each paper is already in the corpus, its relevance scores (question-specific
          and domain-wide), whether it appears to be empirical, and a recommendation
          of SUBMIT, REVIEW, or SKIP. Open this table in Excel or Numbers and work
          through it: confirm SUBMIT papers by reading their abstracts, investigate
          REVIEW papers to resolve the ambiguity, and spot-check a few SKIP papers
          to verify the recommendation is correct.
        </p>
        <p>
          The review table is your ground truth data for building your relevance filter.
          Add a column <code>my_label</code> and fill it in as you review each paper:
          1 (relevant), 0 (not relevant), 0.5 (uncertain). These labels — your own
          considered judgments — are what your filter will be trained and validated against.
        </p>
        <p>
          Then implement your chosen filter approach (see the section below on the three
          options). Run it on all your labeled papers and examine the score distribution.
          Where does it correctly rank relevant papers above non-relevant ones? Where does
          it fail? By end of Week 2: working Python filter, complete manual-label CSV,
          and a first calibration plot.
        </p>
      </div>
    </section>

    <!-- WEEK 3 -->
    <section id="week3">
      <div class="week-header">
        <span class="week-badge">Week 3</span>
        <span class="week-title">Validate, Calibrate, and Complete Your Paper Set</span>
        <span class="week-dates">Days 15–21</span>
      </div>
      <div class="week-body">
        <p>
          This week has two concurrent workstreams. The first is filter validation:
          hold out 10–15 papers from your calibration set, run the filter on them,
          and compute precision, recall, F1, and AUC-ROC comparing filter predictions
          to your manual labels. Examine false positives and false negatives.
          Write a brief analysis of what failure modes reveal about the filter's
          limitations and about the scope of your search question.
        </p>
        <p>
          The second workstream is completing your paper set. If you have ten
          qualifying papers already, verify them: check each DOI against the
          K-ATLAS corpus, confirm the papers are empirical and peer-reviewed,
          confirm each abstract directly addresses the mechanism or outcome
          specified in your question, and collect full-text PDFs. If you do not
          yet have ten qualifying papers, run additional search sessions using
          the query variant strategy described below.
        </p>
        <p>
          Apply your calibrated filter to any new candidate papers found this week —
          this is the practical payoff of building it. Papers that score below your
          calibrated threshold are likely not qualifying; examine them briefly before
          discarding to confirm the filter's recommendation is correct.
        </p>
      </div>
    </section>

    <!-- WEEK 4 -->
    <section id="week4">
      <div class="week-header">
        <span class="week-badge">Week 4</span>
        <span class="week-title">Submit and Reflect</span>
        <span class="week-dates">Days 22–28</span>
      </div>
      <div class="week-body">
        <p>
          Upload your article CSV and PDFs through the K-ATLAS student portal.
          Commit your relevance filter code to the designated repo location.
          Write your reflection report (see the specification below).
        </p>
        <p>
          Attend the lab session in Week 4 prepared to describe, in about five minutes:
          your search question, the most interesting paper you found and why,
          one case where the filter failed and what that reveals about relevance,
          and one thing you would do differently if you were designing the search
          question rather than answering it.
        </p>
      </div>
    </section>

    <!-- FILTER: WHY -->
    <section id="filter-why">
      <h2>The Relevance Filter</h2>
      <h3>Why Build a Filter?</h3>
      <p>
        A relevance filter serves two purposes. Practically, it speeds up your search:
        once calibrated, you can run it on a batch of candidate abstracts in seconds
        and get a ranked list of likely-relevant papers to prioritise for closer reading.
        Intellectually, it makes your relevance criteria explicit and testable.
        Any judgment that can be operationalised can be scrutinised, refined, and improved.
        A filter that performs poorly is not a failure — it is evidence that your
        relevance criteria are underspecified or that the question is ambiguous in ways
        that matter for K-ATLAS's evidence base.
      </p>
      <p>
        More importantly: K-ATLAS needs a relevance filter for every question in its bank.
        Your filter will be integrated as a callable function that the system can invoke
        when new papers arrive and need to be matched to open questions. You are not
        just doing an assignment — you are contributing infrastructure.
      </p>

      <p>
        The full relevance pipeline has three stages. K-ATLAS provides Pass 1 and Pass 3
        as infrastructure (inside <code>process_submitted_pdfs.py</code>). You build Pass 2.
      </p>

      <div class="pipeline">
        <div class="pipe-step" style="border-color:#1a7a4a; background:#eaf7ef;">
          <div class="pipe-step-name" style="color:#1a7a4a;">Pass 1</div>
          <div class="pipe-step-sub">Domain filter<br><em style="font-size:0.66rem;">max sim vs all 130 questions<br>provided by K-ATLAS</em></div>
        </div>
        <div class="pipe-arrow">→</div>
        <div class="pipe-step" style="border-color:#182B49; background:#eef4ff;">
          <div class="pipe-step-name" style="color:#182B49;">Pass 2</div>
          <div class="pipe-step-sub">Question filter<br><em style="font-size:0.66rem;">your calibrated filter<br><strong>← you build this</strong></em></div>
        </div>
        <div class="pipe-arrow">→</div>
        <div class="pipe-step" style="border-color:#7a5800; background:#fff8e6;">
          <div class="pipe-step-name" style="color:#7a5800;">Pass 3</div>
          <div class="pipe-step-sub">Empirical detector<br><em style="font-size:0.66rem;">full-text heuristic<br>provided by K-ATLAS</em></div>
        </div>
        <div class="pipe-arrow">→</div>
        <div class="pipe-step">
          <div class="pipe-step-name">Verdict</div>
          <div class="pipe-step-sub">SUBMIT / REVIEW / SKIP</div>
        </div>
      </div>

      <p>
        Pass 1 checks domain membership: does this paper belong to the field of
        environmental psychology / built environment research? It does this by computing
        the maximum cosine similarity of the paper's abstract against the embeddings
        of all 130 K-ATLAS search questions. A paper scoring below 0.28 on this measure
        is almost certainly outside the domain — an economics paper cited in a footnote,
        a methods paper on a different topic, etc.
      </p>
      <p>
        Pass 2 is your contribution: the question-specific filter calibrated to your
        assigned question. Papers that pass the domain filter but score below your
        calibrated threshold on question similarity are in the right field but answering
        a different question. The system flags them as potentially useful for other
        K-ATLAS questions — your Pass 2 output includes a <code>best_other_question</code>
        field showing which question they best match.
      </p>
      <p>
        Pass 3 checks whether the paper is empirical. It runs on the full PDF text
        (not just the abstract), looking for methods-section language: sample sizes,
        statistical terms, data collection descriptions, physiological measure mentions.
        A paper that passes Passes 1 and 2 but fails Pass 3 is flagged REVIEW rather
        than SUBMIT — it may be a theoretical essay, a commentary, or a review article
        that does not count as one of your ten empirical papers.
      </p>
    </section>

    <!-- FILTER: APPROACHES -->
    <section id="filter-approaches">
      <h3>Three Approaches</h3>
      <p>
        You must choose one of the following three approaches. They increase in
        sophistication and in the quality of results you can expect. The third is
        recommended unless you have no prior Python experience.
      </p>

      <div class="approach-grid">
        <div class="approach-card">
          <div class="approach-name">Approach A</div>
          <div class="approach-badge ab-easy">Baseline</div>
          <div class="approach-desc">
            <strong>Concept keyword matching.</strong> Manually extract 8–12 key concepts
            from the search question (mechanism terms, theory names, outcome words,
            environmental features). Check how many appear in each abstract.
            Score = fraction present. Simple and transparent; brittle to synonyms
            and paraphrase. Use as a baseline comparison if implementing Approach C.
          </div>
        </div>
        <div class="approach-card">
          <div class="approach-name">Approach B</div>
          <div class="approach-badge ab-med">Intermediate</div>
          <div class="approach-desc">
            <strong>TF-IDF cosine similarity.</strong> Represent the query as a
            pseudo-document; fit a TF-IDF vectorizer on all abstracts plus the query;
            compute cosine similarity between query vector and each abstract vector.
            No GPU needed; captures term weighting; still misses semantically equivalent
            terms using different vocabulary.
          </div>
        </div>
        <div class="approach-card">
          <div class="approach-name">Approach C</div>
          <div class="approach-badge ab-hard">Recommended</div>
          <div class="approach-desc">
            <strong>Sentence-transformer embedding similarity.</strong> Use a pre-trained
            sentence transformer (all-MiniLM-L6-v2, ~80 MB) to embed both the query
            and each abstract into a shared semantic space; compute cosine similarity.
            Captures paraphrase and synonym relationships. Runs on CPU in under
            a second per abstract.
          </div>
        </div>
      </div>
    </section>

    <!-- FILTER: IMPLEMENTATION -->
    <section id="filter-implement">
      <h3>Implementation Guide</h3>

      <h4>Setup (Approach C — recommended)</h4>
      <div class="code-block">
        pip install sentence-transformers scikit-learn numpy pandas
      </div>

      <h4>Core filter function</h4>
      <div class="code-block">
        from sentence_transformers import SentenceTransformer
        import numpy as np

        # Load once; reuse across calls (slow to load, fast to run)
        _model = SentenceTransformer('all-MiniLM-L6-v2')

        def compute_relevance(question: str, paper: dict,
                              threshold: float = 0.38) -> dict:
            """
            Args:
                question : the search question (string)
                paper    : dict with keys 'title', 'abstract'
                threshold: cosine similarity cutoff (calibrated per question)
            Returns:
                {
                    'score'    : float,  # cosine similarity in [0, 1]
                    'relevant' : bool,   # True if score >= threshold
                    'method'   : str,    # 'sentence_transformer_MiniLM'
                    'model'    : str     # model name for reproducibility
                }
            """
            # Concatenate title and abstract for richer signal
            paper_text = (paper.get('title', '') + ' ' +
                          paper.get('abstract', '')).strip()

            q_emb = _model.encode(question, normalize_embeddings=True)
            p_emb = _model.encode(paper_text, normalize_embeddings=True)

            score = float(np.dot(q_emb, p_emb))   # cosine sim (vectors are normalised)

            return {
                'score'    : round(score, 4),
                'relevant' : score >= threshold,
                'method'   : 'sentence_transformer_MiniLM',
                'model'    : 'all-MiniLM-L6-v2'
            }
      </div>

      <h4>Batch scoring your candidate pool</h4>
      <div class="code-block">
        import pandas as pd

        df = pd.read_csv('my_candidate_papers.csv')   # must have 'title', 'abstract'
        question = "What peer-reviewed evidence shows that..."   # your question text

        results = [compute_relevance(question, row) for _, row in df.iterrows()]
        df['filter_score']    = [r['score']    for r in results]
        df['filter_relevant'] = [r['relevant'] for r in results]

        # Sort by score descending to see your best candidates first
        df = df.sort_values('filter_score', ascending=False)
        df.to_csv('my_candidates_scored.csv', index=False)

        print(df[['title', 'filter_score', 'filter_relevant',
                   'my_label']].to_string(index=False))
      </div>

      <div class="callout info">
        <div class="callout-title">Approach B — TF-IDF alternative</div>
        <p>If you choose Approach B, use <code>sklearn.feature_extraction.text.TfidfVectorizer</code>
        and <code>sklearn.metrics.pairwise.cosine_similarity</code>. Fit the vectorizer on
        a list containing the question plus all abstracts, then extract the query vector
        (index 0) and compute its cosine similarity with each abstract vector (indices 1+).
        The rest of the calibration and validation pipeline is identical.</p>
      </div>
    </section>

    <!-- FILTER: CALIBRATE -->
    <section id="filter-calibrate">
      <h3>Calibration and Validation</h3>
      <p>
        The default threshold of 0.38 is a starting point — it will not be optimal
        for your specific question. You must calibrate it using your manual labels
        and validate the result on a held-out set. The process is as follows.
      </p>
      <p>
        <em>Calibration set</em>: take your manually labeled papers (minimum 20,
        ideally 25–30; at least 8 labeled 1 and 8 labeled 0). Compute filter scores
        for all of them. Plot precision, recall, and F1 as a function of threshold
        (try thresholds from 0.20 to 0.60 in steps of 0.02). Choose the threshold
        that maximises F1 on the calibration set. This is your calibrated threshold T*.
      </p>
      <p>
        <em>Held-out validation set</em>: hold out 10–15 labeled papers not used
        for calibration. Apply the filter with T* and compute precision, recall, F1,
        and AUC-ROC on this set. This is your honest performance estimate.
      </p>
      <p>
        Report all four metrics in your reflection report. Also produce two figures:
        a precision-recall-F1 vs. threshold curve (calibration), and a scatter plot
        with filter score on the x-axis and your manual label (0/1) on the y-axis,
        with the calibrated threshold shown as a vertical line.
      </p>

      <h4>Computing AUC-ROC</h4>
      <div class="code-block">
        from sklearn.metrics import (precision_score, recall_score,
                                     f1_score, roc_auc_score)
        import numpy as np

        # held_out_labels: list of your manual labels (0 or 1)
        # held_out_scores: list of filter scores for the same papers

        threshold = 0.38   # replace with your calibrated T*

        preds = [1 if s >= threshold else 0 for s in held_out_scores]

        print('Precision:', precision_score(held_out_labels, preds))
        print('Recall:   ', recall_score(held_out_labels, preds))
        print('F1:       ', f1_score(held_out_labels, preds))
        print('AUC-ROC:  ', roc_auc_score(held_out_labels, held_out_scores))
      </div>

      <div class="callout warn">
        <div class="callout-title">Small-sample statistics deserve caution</div>
        <p>With 10–15 papers in the validation set, all statistics will have wide
        confidence intervals. Report exact counts alongside percentages — "3 of 6
        relevant papers were retrieved (recall = 0.50)" is more informative than
        just "recall = 0.50." Interpret your results with appropriate epistemic
        humility, and use the error analysis to make qualitative claims that the
        numbers alone cannot support.</p>
      </div>

      <h4>Error analysis (required)</h4>
      <p>
        List every false positive (filter said relevant, you said not) and every
        false negative (filter said not relevant, you said yes) in the validation set.
        For each case, write one sentence explaining what the filter misunderstood.
        Then write a paragraph synthesising the pattern: what does the filter reliably
        miss, and why? This analysis is worth more to K-ATLAS than the numeric F1 score,
        because it tells the system's maintainers where the filter needs human review.
      </p>
    </section>

    <!-- FILTER: INTEGRATE -->
    <section id="filter-integrate">
      <h3>K-ATLAS Integration</h3>
      <p>
        Package your filter as a module at
        <code>src/services/relevance_filters/{your_question_id}.py</code>.
        The module must export a <code>score()</code> function with the following signature
        exactly — K-ATLAS's ingestion pipeline will call it by name.
      </p>
      <div class="code-block">
        # File: src/services/relevance_filters/SQ_031.py
        # (replace SQ_031 with your question ID)

        QUESTION = "What evidence shows that the design and placement..."
        CALIBRATED_THRESHOLD = 0.41   # your calibrated T*
        MODEL_NAME = 'all-MiniLM-L6-v2'

        def score(paper: dict) -> dict:
            """
            Standard K-ATLAS relevance filter interface.

            Args:
                paper: dict with at minimum 'title' (str) and 'abstract' (str).
                       May also contain 'year', 'doi', 'authors'.
            Returns:
                {
                    'question_id'  : str,   # e.g. 'SQ-031'
                    'score'        : float, # cosine similarity in [0, 1]
                    'relevant'     : bool,  # True if score >= CALIBRATED_THRESHOLD
                    'threshold'    : float, # CALIBRATED_THRESHOLD (for auditability)
                    'method'       : str,   # approach used
                    'validation_f1': float  # F1 on held-out validation set
                }
            """
            ...   # your implementation here
      </div>
      <p>
        Also add a JSON entry to <code>data/relevance_filters/filter_registry.json</code>
        with the following fields: <code>question_id</code>, <code>question_text</code>
        (first 120 characters), <code>module_path</code>, <code>calibrated_threshold</code>,
        <code>validation_f1</code>, <code>validation_n</code>, <code>approach</code>
        (one of: <code>keyword</code>, <code>tfidf</code>, <code>sentence_transformer</code>),
        <code>model_name</code>, <code>known_failure_modes</code> (one sentence).
      </p>
    </section>

    <!-- SEARCH LOG -->

      <p>

        encountered, whether or not it ended up in your final set. This CSV uses
        the standard K-ATLAS format plus two additional columns:
        <code>my_label</code> (1 / 0 / 0.5) and <code>inclusion_decision</code>
        (included / excluded / pending) with a <code>exclusion_reason</code>

        not_relevant / pdf_unavailable / other).
      </p>

    <!-- QUERY VARIANTS -->
    <section id="query-variants">
      <h2>Query Variant Strategy</h2>
      <p>
        If your primary assigned query does not yield ten qualifying papers,
        apply systematic variations. Each variant probes a different sector
        of the literature. Document every variant in your search log with
        the <code>query_variant</code> field.
      </p>

      <h4>Variant 1: Change the evidence type signal</h4>
      <p>
        Replace the evidence type phrase with a different method.
        If the original asks for "experimental studies," try "meta-analyses,"
        "post-occupancy evaluations," "longitudinal studies," or "neuroimaging evidence."
        Different methods address the same question from different angles and appear
        in different papers.
      </p>

      <h4>Variant 2: Change the population or context</h4>
      <p>
        If the original specifies "office workers," try "university students,"
        "hospital patients," "urban residents," or "adults generally."
        Many environmental psychology findings are studied across multiple populations
        and the papers may not appear in searches tied to a specific group.
      </p>

      <h4>Variant 3: Change the theoretical anchor</h4>
      <p>
        If the original anchors to ART, try SRT, biophilia hypothesis,
        or prospect-refuge theory. Different theoretical communities produce
        papers that address the same empirical question under different names.
      </p>

      <h4>Variant 4: Decompose the question</h4>
      <p>
        If your question asks about both a mechanism and an outcome,
        search for each separately. A paper might not address the full
        question but might address the mechanism half — and still qualify
        if the abstract is directly relevant to the mechanism the question specifies.
      </p>

      <h4>Variant 5: Citation chaining via Connected Papers</h4>
      <p>
        Take the most relevant paper found so far and paste its title into
        Connected Papers (connectedpapers.com). The resulting graph shows
        papers that cite it or are cited by it — a productive way to find
        closely related work that shares neither your terminology nor your
        theoretical framing but addresses the same empirical question.
      </p>
    </section>

    <!-- TOOLS -->
    <section id="tools">
      <h2>Using Elicit, Consensus, and Connected Papers</h2>

      <h3>Elicit (elicit.org)</h3>
      <p>
        Elicit is particularly useful for two tasks. First, abstract screening:
        paste your query and Elicit returns papers with structured summaries
        (sample size, methods, key findings, effect sizes) that let you assess
        relevance faster than reading abstracts. Second, extraction: once you
        have your ten papers, use Elicit's extraction table to pull structured
        data — this will partially fill your CSV's abstract field and note column.
      </p>
      <p>
        Elicit works best with research questions phrased as Google AI Citation
        queries (complete sentences, not Boolean). The results overlap with but
        do not duplicate Google AI Citation — typically 30–50% of Elicit's
        results are not in Google's top results.
      </p>

      <h3>Consensus (consensus.app)</h3>
      <p>
        Consensus synthesises evidence across papers and presents "consensus
        claims" — statements supported by a specified percentage of relevant
        papers. It is useful for quickly locating core papers in a well-studied
        area, and for confirming that your question is genuinely contested
        (if Consensus shows high agreement, the IC→DC update your papers can
        produce is large; if it shows mixed evidence, your papers are
        especially valuable for resolving uncertainty).
      </p>

      <h3>Connected Papers (connectedpapers.com)</h3>
      <p>
        Enter the DOI or title of the single most relevant paper you find.
        Connected Papers builds a visual graph of papers with similar references
        and citation relationships. Papers in the same "cluster" as your seed
        paper are strong candidates. Papers on the periphery of the cluster
        may represent adjacent theoretical traditions — worth examining,
        but apply the relevance filter before adding to your shortlist.
      </p>

      <div class="callout good">
        <div class="callout-title">Suggested multi-tool workflow</div>
        <p>Run Google AI Citation first (primary). For each paper found, check Elicit
        for structured extraction. Use Consensus to confirm theoretical framing.
        Take your best paper and run Connected Papers to expand into adjacent literature.
        Apply your relevance filter to all candidates before deciding inclusion. Document
        every step in your search log.</p>
      </div>
    </section>

    <!-- DUPLICATE CHECK -->
    <section id="duplicate-check">
      <h2>Duplicate Checking</h2>
      <p>
        A paper already in the K-ATLAS corpus does not count toward your ten qualifying
        papers. Before adding any paper to your final set, check its DOI against the corpus.
        There are two ways to do this.
      </p>
      <p>
        From your student assignment page, use the built-in duplicate checker: paste the DOI
        into the check field and the system will return "already in corpus" or "not found."
        Alternatively, from a terminal with access to the K-ATLAS database:
      </p>
      <div class="code-block">
        python3 scripts/check_duplicate.py --doi "https://doi.org/10.xxxx/xxx"
        # Returns: FOUND (already in corpus) or NOT FOUND (eligible)
      </div>
      <p>
        If a paper is already in the corpus, it still appears in your search log
        and candidate CSV with <code>exclusion_reason = already_in_corpus</code>.
        Do not discard it from your records — it is evidence that your search is
        finding the right literature, even if the paper itself does not count.
      </p>
    </section>

        across all article finder submissions — aggregate data across all students
        will be used to improve the default threshold for each question.</p>

    <!-- REFLECTION REPORT -->
    <section id="reflection">
      <h2>Reflection Report</h2>
      <p>
        The reflection report is a 4–6 page academic document (APA format,
        with references) covering the following five sections. It is not a diary
        entry — it is a structured scientific account of your methodology,
        findings, and analysis.
      </p>
      <p>
        <strong>Section 1 — The Question and Its Scope.</strong> Restate your assigned
        question and explain what theoretical claims it is designed to support in K-ATLAS.
        What kind of evidence would most directly answer it? What evidence would be
        relevant but insufficient? What evidence would be clearly out of scope?
        This section establishes the conceptual frame that justifies your inclusion
        and exclusion decisions.
      </p>
      <p>
        <strong>Section 2 — Search Methodology.</strong> Summarise your search process:
        how many sessions, which tools, which query variants, and how many candidates
        each session yielded. Include a table derived from your search log. Describe
        any systematic gaps you noticed — topics adjacent to your question that the
        literature treats sparsely, or terminology communities that do not appear
        in each other's citation trails.
      </p>
      <p>
        <strong>Section 3 — Relevance Filter.</strong> Describe your implementation approach,
        justify your choice, report your calibration procedure, and present your
        validation results (all four metrics plus the two required figures). Your
        error analysis — the detailed examination of false positives and false negatives
        — must be here. Conclude with a statement about what the filter's failure modes
        reveal about the boundaries of your question's scope.
      </p>
      <p>
        <strong>Section 4 — The Ten Papers.</strong> Present your ten qualifying papers
        in a summary table (author, year, title, DOI, one-sentence finding). Then discuss
        in prose: which two or three papers are most directly relevant and why; whether the
        papers converge on a single answer to your question or whether they present
        conflicting evidence; and what the set of papers tells us about the state of
        evidence on this question (strong / mixed / emerging / under-studied).
      </p>
      <p>
        <strong>Section 5 — Contribution to K-ATLAS.</strong> Estimate qualitatively what
        IC-to-DC update your ten papers might enable. Which warrants in K-ATLAS's knowledge
        base does this evidence support? Are there papers in your set that speak to
        neural or physiological mechanisms — the highest-value evidence type for strong
        credence updates? End with one paragraph on what you would search for next if
        you had another week.
      </p>
    </section>

<script>
const tocLinks = document.querySelectorAll('.toc-list a');
const observer = new IntersectionObserver(entries => {
  entries.forEach(entry => {
    if (entry.isIntersecting) {
      const id = entry.target.id;
      tocLinks.forEach(a => {
        a.classList.toggle('active', a.getAttribute('href') === '#' + id);
      });
    }
  });
}, { rootMargin: '-10% 0px -75% 0px' });

document.querySelectorAll('[id]').forEach(el => observer.observe(el));
tocLinks.forEach(a => {
  a.addEventListener('click', e => {
    e.preventDefault();
    const t = document.querySelector(a.getAttribute('href'));
    if (t) t.scrollIntoView({ behavior: 'smooth', block: 'start' });
  });
});
</script>

    function shuffle(arr) {
      for (var i = arr.length - 1; i > 0; i--) {
        var j = Math.floor(Math.random() * (i + 1));
        var t = arr[i]; arr[i] = arr[j]; arr[j] = t;
      }
      return arr;
    }
